ol{counter-reset:listitem}.mw-parser-output .hlist ol>li{counter-increment:listitem}.mw-parser-output .hlist ol>li::before{content:" "counter(listitem)"\a0 "}.mw-parser-output .hlist dd ol>li:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist dt ol>li:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist li ol>li:first-child::before{content:" ("counter(listitem)"\a0 "}</style><div class="hlist"><ul><li>piano</li><li>vocal soloists</li><li>mixed chorus</li><li>orchestra</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #eee; color:inherit;">Premiere</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Date</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808">22 December 1808</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Location</th><td class="infobox-data location"><a href="/wiki/Theater_an_der_Wien" title="Theater an der Wien">Theater an der Wien</a>, Vienna</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Performers</th><td class="infobox-data">Ludwig van Beethoven (piano)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i><b>Fantasy</b></i> for piano, vocal soloists, mixed chorus, and orchestra, <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">Op.</a>&#160;80, usually called the <i><b>Choral Fantasy</b></i>, was composed in 1808 by then 38-year-old <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>.
</p><p>Beethoven intended the <i>Fantasy</i> to serve as the concluding work for the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808">benefit concert</a> he put on for himself on 22 December 1808; the performers consisted of vocal soloists, mixed chorus, an orchestra, and Beethoven himself as piano soloist. The <i>Fantasy</i> was designed to include all the participants in the program and thus unites all of these musical forces.
</p><p>The work is noted as a precursor to the later <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background,_composition,_and_premiere"><span id="Background.2C_composition.2C_and_premiere"></span>Background, composition, and premiere</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background, composition, and premiere"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <i>Fantasia</i> was first performed at the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808"><i>Akademie</i> of 22 December 1808</a>, a benefit concert which also saw the premieres of the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Sixth Symphonies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._4_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 4 (Beethoven)">Fourth Piano Concerto</a> as well as a performance of excerpts of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_C_major_(Beethoven)" title="Mass in C major (Beethoven)">Mass in C major</a>. To conclude this memorable concert program, Beethoven wanted a "brilliant finale" that would unite in a single piece the different musical elements highlighted in the concert night: piano solo, mixed chorus and orchestra. The <i>Fantasia</i>, Op. 80, written shortly before, was thus composed expressly to fulfil this role. Beethoven himself played the piano part and the opening solo offers an example of his improvisational style (at the premiere he did, in fact, improvise this section).
</p><p>Beethoven wrote the piece during the second half of December 1808 in an unusually short time by his standards. He commissioned a poet—whose identity is disputed—to write the words shortly before the performance to fit the already written parts. According to Beethoven's pupil <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a>, the poet was <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christoph_Kuffner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christoph Kuffner (page does not exist)">Christoph Kuffner</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christoph_Kuffner" class="extiw" title="de:Christoph Kuffner">de</a>&#93;</span>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but the later Beethoven scholar <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Nottebohm" title="Gustav Nottebohm">Gustav Nottebohm</a> doubted this attribution and suggested it may have been <a href="/wiki/Georg_Friedrich_Treitschke" title="Georg Friedrich Treitschke">Georg Friedrich Treitschke</a>, who in 1814 prepared the final text of Beethoven's opera <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The premiere performance seems to have been a rather troubled one; according to the composer's secretary, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Schindler" title="Anton Schindler">Anton Schindler</a>, it "simply fell apart", a result most likely attributable to insufficient rehearsal time. Because of a mistake in the execution of the piece, it was stopped halfway through and restarted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_von_Seyfried" title="Ignaz von Seyfried">Ignaz von Seyfried</a>'s words:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When the master brought out his orchestral <i>Fantasia</i> with choruses, he arranged with me at the somewhat hurried rehearsal, with wet<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> voice-parts as usual, that the second variation should be played without repeat. In the evening, however, absorbed in his creation, he forgot all about the instructions which he had given, repeated the first part while the orchestra accompanied the second, which sounded not altogether edifying. A trifle too late, the Concertmaster, Unrath, noticed the mistake, looked in surprise at his lost companions, stopped playing and called out dryly: 'Again!' A little displeased, the violinist <a href="/wiki/Anton%C3%ADn_Vranick%C3%BD" title="Antonín Vranický">Anton Wranitzky</a> asked 'With repeats?' 'Yes', came the answer, and now the thing went straight as a string.</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="The_Choral_Fantasy_and_the_Ninth_Symphony">The <i>Choral Fantasy</i> and the Ninth Symphony</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Choral Fantasy and the Ninth Symphony"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The work includes a sequence of variations on a theme that is widely felt to be an early version of a far better known variation theme, namely the one to which Beethoven set the words of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy" title="Ode to Joy">Ode to Joy</a>" in his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>. The two themes are compared below.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score noresize" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.png" width="373" height="66" alt="&#10;\new Score {&#10;  \new Staff {&#10;    \relative c&#39;&#39; {&#10;      \time 2/4&#10;      \key c \major&#10;      \clef treble&#10;      \tempo 4 = 88&#10;      \omit Score.MetronomeMark&#10;        \partial 4 e8^\markup {  \halign #-0.5 &quot;Choral Fantasy&quot;} e f( e d c) c( b a b) c( c d e) e( d)&#10;    }&#10;  }&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score noresize" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.png" width="401" height="66" alt="&#10;\new Score {&#10;  \new Staff {&#10;    \relative c {&#10;      \time 4/4&#10;      \key d \major&#10;      \clef bass&#10;      \tempo 2 = 60&#10;      \omit Score.MetronomeMark&#10;        fis2^\markup {  \halign #-0.5 &quot;Ninth Symphony&quot;}( g4 a) | a4( g fis e) | d2( e4 fis) | fis4.( e8) e2&#10;    }&#10;  }&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<p>Michael Broyles has suggested another musical similarity: the two works share essentially the same harmonic sequence at their climactic moments, the chords (in <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a>) C–F–D–(G)–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>, where the E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> stands out from its harmonic context and is performed <a href="/wiki/Fortissimo" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortissimo">fortissimo</a>. The words sung at this point are (for the <i>Choral Fantasy</i>) "Lieb und Kraft" ("love and strength") and (for the Ninth Symphony) "Über'm Sternenzelt! Über Sternen muss er wohnen." ("Above the tent of the stars, above the stars he must dwell.")<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are also affinities in the texts. The theme of the <i>Choral Fantasy</i> text – universal fraternity with the meeting of arts – evokes similar feelings as the "Ode to Joy" text.
</p><p>Beethoven himself acknowledged the kinship of the two works. In a letter of 1824, when he was writing the Ninth Symphony, he described his project as "a setting of the words of Schiller's immortal '<span title="German-language text"><span lang="de" style="font-style: normal;">Lied an die Freude</span></span>' in the same way as my pianoforte fantasia with chorus, but on a far grander scale."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Choral Fantasy</i> theme is itself taken from an earlier work by Beethoven: it is a slightly modified version of the composer's "<a href="/wiki/Seufzer_eines_Ungeliebten_%E2%80%93_Gegenliebe" title="Seufzer eines Ungeliebten – Gegenliebe">Seufzer eines Ungeliebten – Gegenliebe</a>,” a <a href="/wiki/Lied" title="Lied">lied</a> for high voice and piano written c. 1794–1795.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Form">Form</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Form"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1316064257" /><table class="infobox" style="clear: right; float:right;margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="font-size:115%">External audio</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="text-align: left"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_arts.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="audio icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/20px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/40px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/ABeethovenChoralFantasy2009"><i>Choral Fantasy</i> performed</a> by the Dexter Community Orchestra and the Washtenaw Community Orchestra Chorus in 2009</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i>Choral Fantasy</i>, which lasts about twenty minutes, is divided into two <a href="/wiki/Movement_(music)" title="Movement (music)">movements</a>, played without a break:
</p>
<div><ol style="list-style-type:upper-roman"><li><a href="/wiki/Tempo#Basic_tempo_markings" title="Tempo">Adagio</a></li><li>Finale. <a href="/wiki/Allegro_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Allegro (music)">Allegro</a> – Meno allegro (Allegretto) – Allegro molto – Adagio ma non troppo – <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">Marcia</a>, assai vivace – Allegro – Allegretto ma non troppo quasi andante con moto <i>Schmeichelnd hold und lieblich klingen</i> – <a href="/wiki/Tempo#Basic_tempo_markings" title="Tempo">Presto</a></li></ol></div>
<p>The piece is scored for solo <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>, mixed <a href="/wiki/Choir" title="Choir">chorus</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">soprano</a> soloists, an <a href="/wiki/Alto" title="Alto">alto</a> soloist, two <a href="/wiki/Tenor" title="Tenor">tenor</a> soloists, a <a href="/wiki/Bass_(voice_type)" title="Bass (voice type)">bass</a> soloist, and an orchestra consisting of two <a href="/wiki/Western_concert_flute" title="Western concert flute">flutes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinets</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoons</a>, two <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">horns</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timpani" title="Timpani">timpani</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_section" title="String section">strings</a>.
</p><p>The <i>Fantasy</i> opens with a slow but virtuosic 26-bar piano introduction, beginning in <a href="/wiki/C_minor" title="C minor">C minor</a> and modulating through a variety of keys in florid, improvisatory fashion. At its midpoint it settles on the dominant of the dominant, <a href="/wiki/G_major" title="G major">G major</a>, with an extended cadenza. The implied key is never confirmed and the music eventually returns to C minor in a stormy passage, which at the last moment turns once more toward the dominant seventh of G in a sweeping arpeggio. This concludes the opening section.
</p><p>The main part of the piece, marked "Finale", begins with a march-like motive (<i>Allegro)</i> played by the <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cellos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">basses</a>, alternating with recitative-like interjections from the piano. The music eventually brightens into <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a> and the solo piano introduces the principal theme (<i>meno allegro)</i> discussed above. Variations on the theme are then played by gradually increasing contingents of instruments: a solo flute, two oboes, a trio of two clarinets and bassoon, and string quartet. A full orchestral version of the theme played at a <i><a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(music)" title="Dynamics (music)">forte</a></i> dynamic leads to the re-entry of the piano and to what seems at first like a postlude to this variation set, but that once again turns toward the dominant. The music pauses with a cadenza on the dominant seventh for the solo piano.
</p><p>There is an abrupt change of mood as the minor mode returns in a stormy <i>Allegro molto</i>. The alternating phrases for piano and orchestra are a disguised variation of the principal theme. In fact, the three sections that follow the first variation set form another, larger-scale set of variations. The <i>allegro molto</i> soon takes on a developmental quality with a series of modulations, the strings playing phrases of the theme accompanied by rapid broken chords on the solo piano. After another cadenza on an <a href="/wiki/E_major" title="E major">E major</a> triad the second large variation (<i>Adagio, ma non troppo</i>) follows, a calm, flowing <a href="/wiki/A_major" title="A major">A major</a> section prominently featuring the clarinets. This ends with a call-and-response section between double reeds, horn, and piano, and leads without break into the key of <a href="/wiki/F_major" title="F major">F major</a> and the third variation on the main theme, <i>Marcia</i> (<i>assai vivace</i>). A reprise of the instrumental theme from the first <i>Allegro</i> forms the transition into the choral finale (<i>Allegretto</i>).
</p><p>The first half of this is essentially a recapitulation of previously heard material at the beginning of the <i>Finale</i> with the addition of solo voices and chorus, another point of similarity with the finale of the Ninth Symphony. After a prolonged dominant pedal and arpeggios from the soloist the chorus, two solo sopranos sing the main theme, followed by a variation featuring a trio of men's voices. After these two variations the entire chorus is joined by the orchestra for the first time in a <i><a href="/wiki/Tutti" title="Tutti">tutti</a></i> rendition of the theme. The music gains excitement and eventually breaks into an accelerated coda (<i>Presto</i>) with all forces joining to bring the piece to a triumphant close.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Text">Text</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Text"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div lang="de" style="float:left; margin-left: 1em;" class="poem">
<p>Schmeichelnd hold und lieblich klingen<br />
unseres Lebens Harmonien,<br />
und dem Schönheitssinn entschwingen<br />
Blumen sich, die ewig blühn.<br />
Fried und Freude gleiten freundlich<br />
wie der Wellen Wechselspiel.<br />
Was sich drängte rauh und feindlich,<br />
ordnet sich zu Hochgefühl.<br />
<br />
Wenn der Töne Zauber walten<br />
und des Wortes Weihe spricht,<br />
muss sich Herrliches gestalten,<br />
Nacht und Stürme werden Licht.<br />
Äuß're Ruhe, inn're Wonne<br />
herrschen für den Glücklichen.<br />
Doch der Künste Frühlingssonne<br />
läßt aus beiden Licht entstehn.<br />
<br />
Großes, das ins Herz gedrungen,<br />
blüht dann neu und schön empor.<br />
Hat ein Geist sich aufgeschwungen,<br />
hallt ihm stets ein Geisterchor.<br />
Nehmt denn hin, ihr schönen Seelen,<br />
froh die Gaben schöner Kunst<br />
Wenn sich Lieb und Kraft vermählen,<br />
lohnt den Menschen Göttergunst.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NC&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div>
<div style="margin-left:1em; float:left;" class="poem">
<p>Graceful, charming and sweet is the sound<br />
Of our life's harmonies,<br />
and from a sense of beauty arise<br />
Flowers which eternally bloom.<br />
Peace and joy advance in perfect concord,<br />
like the changing play of the waves.<br />
All that was harsh and hostile,<br />
has turned into sublime delight.<br />
<br />
When music's enchantment reigns,<br />
speaking of the sacred word,<br />
Magnificence takes form,<br />
The night and the tempest turns to light:<br />
Outer peace and inner bliss<br />
Reign o'er the fortunate ones.<br />
All art in the spring's sun<br />
Lets light flow from both.<br />
<br />
Greatness, once it has pierced the heart,<br />
Then blooms anew in all its beauty.<br />
Once one's being has taken flight,<br />
A choir of spirits resounds in response.<br />
Accept then, you beautiful souls,<br />
Joyously the gifts of high art.<br />
When love and strength are united,<br />
Divine grace is bestowed upon Man.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NC&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div><div style="clear:left;" class=""></div>
<p>The piece ends with repetition of phrases from the last four lines.
</p><p>As noted above, the words were written in haste, and Beethoven was perhaps not entirely pleased with them. He later wrote to his publisher <a href="/wiki/Breitkopf_%26_H%C3%A4rtel" title="Breitkopf &amp; Härtel">Breitkopf &amp; Härtel</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>You may wish to print another text, as the text like the music was written very quickly ... Still with another set of words I want the word <i>kraft</i> ["strength"] to be kept or one similar to it in its place.</p></blockquote>
<p>As Kalischer et al. observe, the word <i>Kraft</i> "is treated with grand style in the music."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A new German text was written by a German poet and Communist politician <a href="/wiki/Johannes_R._Becher" title="Johannes R. Becher">Johannes R. Becher</a> in 1951, keeping the word <i>Kraft</i> in the same position. Becher's text, inspired by Beethoven's explicit permission to change the lyrics, is an ode to peace that reflects the post-war atmosphere. Becher's lyrics were used in several recordings especially in <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, for example those of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Konwitschny" title="Franz Konwitschny">Franz Konwitschny</a> or <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kegel" title="Herbert Kegel">Herbert Kegel</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

(1996, 10) identify Kuffner (1780–1846) as "an official at the War Ministry, musically talented poet, author of the text of the Choral Fantasia Op. 80, and of the drama <i>Tarpeja</i>, for which Beethoven supplied incidental music WoO 2, in 1813."</span>

					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical composition by Ludwig van Beethoven</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316064257">.mw-parser-output December 1808">22 December 1808</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Location</th><td class="infobox-data location"><a href="/wiki/Theater_an_der_Wien" title="Theater an der Wien">Theater an der Wien</a>, Vienna</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Performers</th><td class="infobox-data">Ludwig van Beethoven (piano)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i><b>Fantasy</b></i> for piano, vocal soloists, mixed chorus, and orchestra, <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">Op.</a>&#160;80, usually called the <i><b>Choral Fantasy</b></i>, was composed in 1808 by then 38-year-old <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>.
</p><p>Beethoven intended the <i>Fantasy</i> to serve as the concluding work for the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808">benefit concert</a> he put on for himself on 22 December 1808; the performers consisted of vocal soloists, mixed chorus, an orchestra, and Beethoven himself as piano soloist. The <i>Fantasy</i> was designed to include all the participants in the program and thus unites all of these musical forces.
</p><p>The work is noted as a precursor to the later <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background,_composition,_and_premiere"><span id="Background.2C_composition.2C_and_premiere"></span>Background, composition, and premiere</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background, composition, and premiere"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <i>Fantasia</i> was first performed at the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808"><i>Akademie</i> of 22 December 1808</a>, a benefit concert which also saw the premieres of the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Sixth Symphonies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._4_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 4 (Beethoven)">Fourth Piano Concerto</a> as well as a performance of excerpts of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_C_major_(Beethoven)" title="Mass in C major (Beethoven)">Mass in C major</a>. To conclude this memorable concert program, Beethoven wanted a "brilliant finale" that would unite in a single piece the different musical elements highlighted in the concert night: piano solo, mixed chorus and orchestra. The <i>Fantasia</i>, Op. 80, written shortly before, was thus composed expressly to fulfil this role. Beethoven himself played the piano part and the opening solo offers an example of his improvisational style (at the premiere he did, in fact, improvise this section).
</p><p>Beethoven wrote the piece during the second half of December 1808 in an unusually short time by his standards. He commissioned a poet—whose identity is disputed—to write the words shortly before the performance to fit the already written parts. According to Beethoven's pupil <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a>, the poet was <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christoph_Kuffner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christoph Kuffner (page does not exist)">Christoph Kuffner</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christoph_Kuffner" class="extiw" title="de:Christoph Kuffner">de</a>&#93;</span>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but the later Beethoven scholar <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Nottebohm" title="Gustav Nottebohm">Gustav Nottebohm</a> doubted this attribution and suggested it may have been <a href="/wiki/Georg_Friedrich_Treitschke" title="Georg Friedrich Treitschke">Georg Friedrich Treitschke</a>, who in 1814 prepared the final text of Beethoven's opera <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The premiere performance seems to have been a rather troubled one; according to the composer's secretary, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Schindler" title="Anton Schindler">Anton Schindler</a>, it "simply fell apart", a result most likely attributable to insufficient rehearsal time. Because of a mistake in the execution of the piece, it was stopped halfway through and restarted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_von_Seyfried" title="Ignaz von Seyfried">Ignaz von Seyfried</a>'s words:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When the master brought out his orchestral <i>Fantasia</i> with choruses, he arranged with me at the somewhat hurried rehearsal, with wet<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> voice-parts as usual, that the second variation should be played without repeat. In the evening, however, absorbed in his creation, he forgot all about the instructions which he had given, repeated the first part while the orchestra accompanied the second, which sounded not altogether edifying. A trifle too late, the Concertmaster, Unrath, noticed the mistake, looked in surprise at his lost companions, stopped playing and called out dryly: 'Again!' A little displeased, the violinist <a href="/wiki/Anton%C3%ADn_Vranick%C3%BD" title="Antonín Vranický">Anton Wranitzky</a> asked 'With repeats?' 'Yes', came the answer, and now the thing went straight as a string.</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="The_Choral_Fantasy_and_the_Ninth_Symphony">The <i>Choral Fantasy</i> and the Ninth Symphony</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Choral Fantasy and the Ninth Symphony"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The work includes a sequence of variations on a theme that is widely felt to be an early version of a far better known variation theme, namely the one to which Beethoven set the words of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy" title="Ode to Joy">Ode to Joy</a>" in his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>. The two themes are compared below.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score noresize" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.png" width="373" height="66" alt="&#10;\new Score {&#10;  \new Staff {&#10;    \relative c&#39;&#39; {&#10;      \time 2/4&#10;      \key c \major&#10;      \clef treble&#10;      \tempo 4 = 88&#10;      \omit Score.MetronomeMark&#10;        \partial 4 e8^\markup {  \halign #-0.5 &quot;Choral Fantasy&quot;} e f( e d c) c( b a b) c( c d e) e( d)&#10;    }&#10;  }&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score noresize" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.png" width="401" height="66" alt="&#10;\new Score {&#10;  \new Staff {&#10;    \relative c {&#10;      \time 4/4&#10;      \key d \major&#10;      \clef bass&#10;      \tempo 2 = 60&#10;      \omit Score.MetronomeMark&#10;        fis2^\markup {  \halign #-0.5 &quot;Ninth Symphony&quot;}( g4 a) | a4( g fis e) | d2( e4 fis) | fis4.( e8) e2&#10;    }&#10;  }&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<p>Michael Broyles has suggested another musical similarity: the two works share essentially the same harmonic sequence at their climactic moments, the chords (in <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a>) C–F–D–(G)–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>, where the E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> stands out from its harmonic context and is performed <a href="/wiki/Fortissimo" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortissimo">fortissimo</a>. The words sung at this point are (for the <i>Choral Fantasy</i>) "Lieb und Kraft" ("love and strength") and (for the Ninth Symphony) "Über'm Sternenzelt! Über Sternen muss er wohnen." ("Above the tent of the stars, above the stars he must dwell.")<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are also affinities in the texts. The theme of the <i>Choral Fantasy</i> text – universal fraternity with the meeting of arts – evokes similar feelings as the "Ode to Joy" text.
</p><p>Beethoven himself acknowledged the kinship of the two works. In a letter of 1824, when he was writing the Ninth Symphony, he described his project as "a setting of the words of Schiller's immortal '<span title="German-language text"><span lang="de" style="font-style: normal;">Lied an die Freude</span></span>' in the same way as my pianoforte fantasia with chorus, but on a far grander scale."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Choral Fantasy</i> theme is itself taken from an earlier work by Beethoven: it is a slightly modified version of the composer's "<a href="/wiki/Seufzer_eines_Ungeliebten_%E2%80%93_Gegenliebe" title="Seufzer eines Ungeliebten – Gegenliebe">Seufzer eines Ungeliebten – Gegenliebe</a>,” a <a href="/wiki/Lied" title="Lied">lied</a> for high voice and piano written c. 1794–1795.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Form">Form</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Form"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1316064257" /><table class="infobox" style="clear: right; float:right;margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="font-size:115%">External audio</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="text-align: left"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_arts.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="audio icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/20px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/40px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/ABeethovenChoralFantasy2009"><i>Choral Fantasy</i> performed</a> by the Dexter Community Orchestra and the Washtenaw Community Orchestra Chorus in 2009</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i>Choral Fantasy</i>, which lasts about twenty minutes, is divided into two <a href="/wiki/Movement_(music)" title="Movement (music)">movements</a>, played without a break:
</p>
<div><ol style="list-style-type:upper-roman"><li><a href="/wiki/Tempo#Basic_tempo_markings" title="Tempo">Adagio</a></li><li>Finale. <a href="/wiki/Allegro_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Allegro (music)">Allegro</a> – Meno allegro (Allegretto) – Allegro molto – Adagio ma non troppo – <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">Marcia</a>, assai vivace – Allegro – Allegretto ma non troppo quasi andante con moto <i>Schmeichelnd hold und lieblich klingen</i> – <a href="/wiki/Tempo#Basic_tempo_markings" title="Tempo">Presto</a></li></ol></div>
<p>The piece is scored for solo <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>, mixed <a href="/wiki/Choir" title="Choir">chorus</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">soprano</a> soloists, an <a href="/wiki/Alto" title="Alto">alto</a> soloist, two <a href="/wiki/Tenor" title="Tenor">tenor</a> soloists, a <a href="/wiki/Bass_(voice_type)" title="Bass (voice type)">bass</a> soloist, and an orchestra consisting of two <a href="/wiki/Western_concert_flute" title="Western concert flute">flutes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinets</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoons</a>, two <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">horns</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timpani" title="Timpani">timpani</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_section" title="String section">strings</a>.
</p><p>The <i>Fantasy</i> opens with a slow but virtuosic 26-bar piano introduction, beginning in <a href="/wiki/C_minor" title="C minor">C minor</a> and modulating through a variety of keys in florid, improvisatory fashion. At its midpoint it settles on the dominant of the dominant, <a href="/wiki/G_major" title="G major">G major</a>, with an extended cadenza. The implied key is never confirmed and the music eventually returns to C minor in a stormy passage, which at the last moment turns once more toward the dominant seventh of G in a sweeping arpeggio. This concludes the opening section.
</p><p>The main part of the piece, marked "Finale", begins with a march-like motive (<i>Allegro)</i> played by the <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cellos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">basses</a>, alternating with recitative-like interjections from the piano. The music eventually brightens into <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a> and the solo piano introduces the principal theme (<i>meno allegro)</i> discussed above. Variations on the theme are then played by gradually increasing contingents of instruments: a solo flute, two oboes, a trio of two clarinets and bassoon, and string quartet. A full orchestral version of the theme played at a <i><a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(music)" title="Dynamics (music)">forte</a></i> dynamic leads to the re-entry of the piano and to what seems at first like a postlude to this variation set, but that once again turns toward the dominant. The music pauses with a cadenza on the dominant seventh for the solo piano.
</p><p>There is an abrupt change of mood as the minor mode returns in a stormy <i>Allegro molto</i>. The alternating phrases for piano and orchestra are a disguised variation of the principal theme. In fact, the three sections that follow the first variation set form another, larger-scale set of variations. The <i>allegro molto</i> soon takes on a developmental quality with a series of modulations, the strings playing phrases of the theme accompanied by rapid broken chords on the solo piano. After another cadenza on an <a href="/wiki/E_major" title="E major">E major</a> triad the second large variation (<i>Adagio, ma non troppo</i>) follows, a calm, flowing <a href="/wiki/A_major" title="A major">A major</a> section prominently featuring the clarinets. This ends with a call-and-response section between double reeds, horn, and piano, and leads without break into the key of <a href="/wiki/F_major" title="F major">F major</a> and the third variation on the main theme, <i>Marcia</i> (<i>assai vivace</i>). A reprise of the instrumental theme from the first <i>Allegro</i> forms the transition into the choral finale (<i>Allegretto</i>).
</p><p>The first half of this is essentially a recapitulation of previously heard material at the beginning of the <i>Finale</i> with the addition of solo voices and chorus, another point of similarity with the finale of the Ninth Symphony. After a prolonged dominant pedal and arpeggios from the soloist the chorus, two solo sopranos sing the main theme, followed by a variation featuring a trio of men's voices. After these two variations the entire chorus is joined by the orchestra for the first time in a <i><a href="/wiki/Tutti" title="Tutti">tutti</a></i> rendition of the theme. The music gains excitement and eventually breaks into an accelerated coda (<i>Presto</i>) with all forces joining to bring the piece to a triumphant close.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Text">Text</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Text"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div lang="de" style="float:left; margin-left: 1em;" class="poem">
<p>Schmeichelnd hold und lieblich klingen<br />
unseres Lebens Harmonien,<br />
und dem Schönheitssinn entschwingen<br />
Blumen sich, die ewig blühn.<br />
Fried und Freude gleiten freundlich<br />
wie der Wellen Wechselspiel.<br />
Was sich drängte rauh und feindlich,<br />
ordnet sich zu Hochgefühl.<br />
<br />
Wenn der Töne Zauber walten<br />
und des Wortes Weihe spricht,<br />
muss sich Herrliches gestalten,<br />
Nacht und Stürme werden Licht.<br />
Äuß're Ruhe, inn're Wonne<br />
herrschen für den Glücklichen.<br />
Doch der Künste Frühlingssonne<br />
läßt aus beiden Licht entstehn.<br />
<br />
Großes, das ins Herz gedrungen,<br />
blüht dann neu und schön empor.<br />
Hat ein Geist sich aufgeschwungen,<br />
hallt ihm stets ein Geisterchor.<br />
Nehmt denn hin, ihr schönen Seelen,<br />
froh die Gaben schöner Kunst<br />
Wenn sich Lieb und Kraft vermählen,<br />
lohnt den Menschen Göttergunst.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NC&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div>
<div style="margin-left:1em; float:left;" class="poem">
<p>Graceful, charming and sweet is the sound<br />
Of our life's harmonies,<br />
and from a sense of beauty arise<br />
Flowers which eternally bloom.<br />
Peace and joy advance in perfect concord,<br />
like the changing play of the waves.<br />
All that was harsh and hostile,<br />
has turned into sublime delight.<br />
<br />
When music's enchantment reigns,<br />
speaking of the sacred word,<br />
Magnificence takes form,<br />
The night and the tempest turns to light:<br />
Outer peace and inner bliss<br />
Reign o'er the fortunate ones.<br />
All art in the spring's sun<br />
Lets light flow from both.<br />
<br />
Greatness, once it has pierced the heart,<br />
Then blooms anew in all its beauty.<br />
Once one's being has taken flight,<br />
A choir of spirits resounds in response.<br />
Accept then, you beautiful souls,<br />
Joyously the gifts of high art.<br />
When love and strength are united,<br />
Divine grace is bestowed upon Man.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NC&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div><div style="clear:left;" class=""></div>
<p>The piece ends with repetition of phrases from the last four lines.
</p><p>As noted above, the words were written in haste, and Beethoven was perhaps not entirely pleased with them. He later wrote to his publisher <a href="/wiki/Breitkopf_%26_H%C3%A4rtel" title="Breitkopf &amp; Härtel">Breitkopf &amp; Härtel</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>You may wish to print another text, as the text like the music was written very quickly ... Still with another set of words I want the word <i>kraft</i> ["strength"] to be kept or one similar to it in its place.</p></blockquote>
<p>As Kalischer et al. observe, the word <i>Kraft</i> "is treated with grand style in the music."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A new German text was written by a German poet and Communist politician <a href="/wiki/Johannes_R._Becher" title="Johannes R. Becher">Johannes R. Becher</a> in 1951, keeping the word <i>Kraft</i> in the same position. Becher's text, inspired by Beethoven's explicit permission to change the lyrics, is an ode to peace that reflects the post-war atmosphere. Becher's lyrics were used in several recordings especially in <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, for example those of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Konwitschny" title="Franz Konwitschny">Franz Konwitschny</a> or <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kegel" title="Herbert Kegel">Herbert Kegel</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="References">References</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: References"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical composition by Ludwig van Beethoven</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

ol>li:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist dt ol>li:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist li ol>li:first-child::before{content:" ("counter(listitem)"\a0 "}</style><div class="hlist"><ul><li>piano</li><li>vocal soloists</li><li>mixed chorus</li><li>orchestra</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #eee; color:inherit;">Premiere</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Date</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808">22 December 1808</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Location</th><td class="infobox-data location"><a href="/wiki/Theater_an_der_Wien" title="Theater an der Wien">Theater an der Wien</a>, Vienna</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Performers</th><td class="infobox-data">Ludwig van Beethoven (piano)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i><b>Fantasy</b></i> for piano, vocal soloists, mixed chorus, and orchestra, <a href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">Op.</a>&#160;80, usually called the <i><b>Choral Fantasy</b></i>, was composed in 1808 by then 38-year-old <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>.
</p><p>Beethoven intended the <i>Fantasy</i> to serve as the concluding work for the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808">benefit concert</a> he put on for himself on 22 December 1808; the performers consisted of vocal soloists, mixed chorus, an orchestra, and Beethoven himself as piano soloist. The <i>Fantasy</i> was designed to include all the participants in the program and thus unites all of these musical forces.
</p><p>The work is noted as a precursor to the later <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background,_composition,_and_premiere"><span id="Background.2C_composition.2C_and_premiere"></span>Background, composition, and premiere</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background, composition, and premiere"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <i>Fantasia</i> was first performed at the <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_concert_of_22_December_1808" title="Beethoven concert of 22 December 1808"><i>Akademie</i> of 22 December 1808</a>, a benefit concert which also saw the premieres of the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._5_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 5 (Beethoven)">Fifth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._6_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 6 (Beethoven)">Sixth Symphonies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._4_(Beethoven)" title="Piano Concerto No. 4 (Beethoven)">Fourth Piano Concerto</a> as well as a performance of excerpts of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_C_major_(Beethoven)" title="Mass in C major (Beethoven)">Mass in C major</a>. To conclude this memorable concert program, Beethoven wanted a "brilliant finale" that would unite in a single piece the different musical elements highlighted in the concert night: piano solo, mixed chorus and orchestra. The <i>Fantasia</i>, Op. 80, written shortly before, was thus composed expressly to fulfil this role. Beethoven himself played the piano part and the opening solo offers an example of his improvisational style (at the premiere he did, in fact, improvise this section).
</p><p>Beethoven wrote the piece during the second half of December 1808 in an unusually short time by his standards. He commissioned a poet—whose identity is disputed—to write the words shortly before the performance to fit the already written parts. According to Beethoven's pupil <a href="/wiki/Carl_Czerny" title="Carl Czerny">Carl Czerny</a>, the poet was <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christoph_Kuffner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christoph Kuffner (page does not exist)">Christoph Kuffner</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christoph_Kuffner" class="extiw" title="de:Christoph Kuffner">de</a>&#93;</span>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but the later Beethoven scholar <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Nottebohm" title="Gustav Nottebohm">Gustav Nottebohm</a> doubted this attribution and suggested it may have been <a href="/wiki/Georg_Friedrich_Treitschke" title="Georg Friedrich Treitschke">Georg Friedrich Treitschke</a>, who in 1814 prepared the final text of Beethoven's opera <i><a href="/wiki/Fidelio" title="Fidelio">Fidelio</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The premiere performance seems to have been a rather troubled one; according to the composer's secretary, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Schindler" title="Anton Schindler">Anton Schindler</a>, it "simply fell apart", a result most likely attributable to insufficient rehearsal time. Because of a mistake in the execution of the piece, it was stopped halfway through and restarted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_von_Seyfried" title="Ignaz von Seyfried">Ignaz von Seyfried</a>'s words:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When the master brought out his orchestral <i>Fantasia</i> with choruses, he arranged with me at the somewhat hurried rehearsal, with wet<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> voice-parts as usual, that the second variation should be played without repeat. In the evening, however, absorbed in his creation, he forgot all about the instructions which he had given, repeated the first part while the orchestra accompanied the second, which sounded not altogether edifying. A trifle too late, the Concertmaster, Unrath, noticed the mistake, looked in surprise at his lost companions, stopped playing and called out dryly: 'Again!' A little displeased, the violinist <a href="/wiki/Anton%C3%ADn_Vranick%C3%BD" title="Antonín Vranický">Anton Wranitzky</a> asked 'With repeats?' 'Yes', came the answer, and now the thing went straight as a string.</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="The_Choral_Fantasy_and_the_Ninth_Symphony">The <i>Choral Fantasy</i> and the Ninth Symphony</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Choral Fantasy and the Ninth Symphony"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The work includes a sequence of variations on a theme that is widely felt to be an early version of a far better known variation theme, namely the one to which Beethoven set the words of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy" title="Ode to Joy">Ode to Joy</a>" in his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._9_(Beethoven)" title="Symphony No. 9 (Beethoven)">Ninth Symphony</a>. The two themes are compared below.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score noresize" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.png" width="373" height="66" alt="&#10;\new Score {&#10;  \new Staff {&#10;    \relative c&#39;&#39; {&#10;      \time 2/4&#10;      \key c \major&#10;      \clef treble&#10;      \tempo 4 = 88&#10;      \omit Score.MetronomeMark&#10;        \partial 4 e8^\markup {  \halign #-0.5 &quot;Choral Fantasy&quot;} e f( e d c) c( b a b) c( c d e) e( d)&#10;    }&#10;  }&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/f/r/fr5uupdsq2l3opxotvg7owq8x7ryyn6/fr5uupds.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score noresize" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.midi"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.png" width="401" height="66" alt="&#10;\new Score {&#10;  \new Staff {&#10;    \relative c {&#10;      \time 4/4&#10;      \key d \major&#10;      \clef bass&#10;      \tempo 2 = 60&#10;      \omit Score.MetronomeMark&#10;        fis2^\markup {  \halign #-0.5 &quot;Ninth Symphony&quot;}( g4 a) | a4( g fis e) | d2( e4 fis) | fis4.( e8) e2&#10;    }&#10;  }&#10;}&#10;" /><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" /><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/v/avrpl805z2dzck1ivn885mwqw3lr356/avrpl805.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></dd></dl>
<p>Michael Broyles has suggested another musical similarity: the two works share essentially the same harmonic sequence at their climactic moments, the chords (in <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a>) C–F–D–(G)–E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>, where the E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> stands out from its harmonic context and is performed <a href="/wiki/Fortissimo" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortissimo">fortissimo</a>. The words sung at this point are (for the <i>Choral Fantasy</i>) "Lieb und Kraft" ("love and strength") and (for the Ninth Symphony) "Über'm Sternenzelt! Über Sternen muss er wohnen." ("Above the tent of the stars, above the stars he must dwell.")<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are also affinities in the texts. The theme of the <i>Choral Fantasy</i> text – universal fraternity with the meeting of arts – evokes similar feelings as the "Ode to Joy" text.
</p><p>Beethoven himself acknowledged the kinship of the two works. In a letter of 1824, when he was writing the Ninth Symphony, he described his project as "a setting of the words of Schiller's immortal '<span title="German-language text"><span lang="de" style="font-style: normal;">Lied an die Freude</span></span>' in the same way as my pianoforte fantasia with chorus, but on a far grander scale."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Choral Fantasy</i> theme is itself taken from an earlier work by Beethoven: it is a slightly modified version of the composer's "<a href="/wiki/Seufzer_eines_Ungeliebten_%E2%80%93_Gegenliebe" title="Seufzer eines Ungeliebten – Gegenliebe">Seufzer eines Ungeliebten – Gegenliebe</a>,” a <a href="/wiki/Lied" title="Lied">lied</a> for high voice and piano written c. 1794–1795.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Form">Form</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Form"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1316064257" /><table class="infobox" style="clear: right; float:right;margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="font-size:115%">External audio</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="text-align: left"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_arts.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="audio icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/20px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/40px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/ABeethovenChoralFantasy2009"><i>Choral Fantasy</i> performed</a> by the Dexter Community Orchestra and the Washtenaw Community Orchestra Chorus in 2009</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i>Choral Fantasy</i>, which lasts about twenty minutes, is divided into two <a href="/wiki/Movement_(music)" title="Movement (music)">movements</a>, played without a break:
</p>
<div><ol style="list-style-type:upper-roman"><li><a href="/wiki/Tempo#Basic_tempo_markings" title="Tempo">Adagio</a></li><li>Finale. <a href="/wiki/Allegro_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Allegro (music)">Allegro</a> – Meno allegro (Allegretto) – Allegro molto – Adagio ma non troppo – <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">Marcia</a>, assai vivace – Allegro – Allegretto ma non troppo quasi andante con moto <i>Schmeichelnd hold und lieblich klingen</i> – <a href="/wiki/Tempo#Basic_tempo_markings" title="Tempo">Presto</a></li></ol></div>
<p>The piece is scored for solo <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>, mixed <a href="/wiki/Choir" title="Choir">chorus</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">soprano</a> soloists, an <a href="/wiki/Alto" title="Alto">alto</a> soloist, two <a href="/wiki/Tenor" title="Tenor">tenor</a> soloists, a <a href="/wiki/Bass_(voice_type)" title="Bass (voice type)">bass</a> soloist, and an orchestra consisting of two <a href="/wiki/Western_concert_flute" title="Western concert flute">flutes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinets</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoons</a>, two <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">horns</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timpani" title="Timpani">timpani</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_section" title="String section">strings</a>.
</p><p>The <i>Fantasy</i> opens with a slow but virtuosic 26-bar piano introduction, beginning in <a href="/wiki/C_minor" title="C minor">C minor</a> and modulating through a variety of keys in florid, improvisatory fashion. At its midpoint it settles on the dominant of the dominant, <a href="/wiki/G_major" title="G major">G major</a>, with an extended cadenza. The implied key is never confirmed and the music eventually returns to C minor in a stormy passage, which at the last moment turns once more toward the dominant seventh of G in a sweeping arpeggio. This concludes the opening section.
</p><p>The main part of the piece, marked "Finale", begins with a march-like motive (<i>Allegro)</i> played by the <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cellos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">basses</a>, alternating with recitative-like interjections from the piano. The music eventually brightens into <a href="/wiki/C_major" title="C major">C major</a> and the solo piano introduces the principal theme (<i>meno allegro)</i> discussed above. Variations on the theme are then played by gradually increasing contingents of instruments: a solo flute, two oboes, a trio of two clarinets and bassoon, and string quartet. A full orchestral version of the theme played at a <i><a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(music)" title="Dynamics (music)">forte</a></i> dynamic leads to the re-entry of the piano and to what seems at first like a postlude to this variation set, but that once again turns toward the dominant. The music pauses with a cadenza on the dominant seventh for the solo piano.
</p><p>There is an abrupt change of mood as the minor mode returns in a stormy <i>Allegro molto</i>. The alternating phrases for piano and orchestra are a disguised variation of the principal theme. In fact, the three sections that follow the first variation set form another, larger-scale set of variations. The <i>allegro molto</i> soon takes on a developmental quality with a series of modulations, the strings playing phrases of the theme accompanied by rapid broken chords on the solo piano. After another cadenza on an <a href="/wiki/E_major" title="E major">E major</a> triad the second large variation (<i>Adagio, ma non troppo</i>) follows, a calm, flowing <a href="/wiki/A_major" title="A major">A major</a> section prominently featuring the clarinets. This ends with a call-and-response section between double reeds, horn, and piano, and leads without break into the key of <a href="/wiki/F_major" title="F major">F major</a> and the third variation on the main theme, <i>Marcia</i> (<i>assai vivace</i>). A reprise of the instrumental theme from the first <i>Allegro</i> forms the transition into the choral finale (<i>Allegretto</i>).
</p><p>The first half of this is essentially a recapitulation of previously heard material at the beginning of the <i>Finale</i> with the addition of solo voices and chorus, another point of similarity with the finale of the Ninth Symphony. After a prolonged dominant pedal and arpeggios from the soloist the chorus, two solo sopranos sing the main theme, followed by a variation featuring a trio of men's voices. After these two variations the entire chorus is joined by the orchestra for the first time in a <i><a href="/wiki/Tutti" title="Tutti">tutti</a></i> rendition of the theme. The music gains excitement and eventually breaks into an accelerated coda (<i>Presto</i>) with all forces joining to bring the piece to a triumphant close.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Text">Text</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Text"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div lang="de" style="float:left; margin-left: 1em;" class="poem">
<p>Schmeichelnd hold und lieblich klingen<br />
unseres Lebens Harmonien,<br />
und dem Schönheitssinn entschwingen<br />
Blumen sich, die ewig blühn.<br />
Fried und Freude gleiten freundlich<br />
wie der Wellen Wechselspiel.<br />
Was sich drängte rauh und feindlich,<br />
ordnet sich zu Hochgefühl.<br />
<br />
Wenn der Töne Zauber walten<br />
und des Wortes Weihe spricht,<br />
muss sich Herrliches gestalten,<br />
Nacht und Stürme werden Licht.<br />
Äuß're Ruhe, inn're Wonne<br />
herrschen für den Glücklichen.<br />
Doch der Künste Frühlingssonne<br />
läßt aus beiden Licht entstehn.<br />
<br />
Großes, das ins Herz gedrungen,<br />
blüht dann neu und schön empor.<br />
Hat ein Geist sich aufgeschwungen,<br />
hallt ihm stets ein Geisterchor.<br />
Nehmt denn hin, ihr schönen Seelen,<br />
froh die Gaben schöner Kunst<br />
Wenn sich Lieb und Kraft vermählen,<br />
lohnt den Menschen Göttergunst.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NC&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div>
<div style="margin-left:1em; float:left;" class="poem">
<p>Graceful, charming and sweet is the sound<br />
Of our life's harmonies,<br />
and from a sense of beauty arise<br />
Flowers which eternally bloom.<br />
Peace and joy advance in perfect concord,<br />
like the changing play of the waves.<br />
All that was harsh and hostile,<br />
has turned into sublime delight.<br />
<br />
When music's enchantment reigns,<br />
speaking of the sacred word,<br />
Magnificence takes form,<br />
The night and the tempest turns to light:<br />
Outer peace and inner bliss<br />
Reign o'er the fortunate ones.<br />
All art in the spring's sun<br />
Lets light flow from both.<br />
<br />
Greatness, once it has pierced the heart,<br />
Then blooms anew in all its beauty.<br />
Once one's being has taken flight,<br />
A choir of spirits resounds in response.<br />
Accept then, you beautiful souls,<br />
Joyously the gifts of high art.<br />
When love and strength are united,<br />
Divine grace is bestowed upon Man.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NC&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div><div style="clear:left;" class=""></div>
<p>The piece ends with repetition of phrases from the last four lines.
</p><p>As noted above, the words were written in haste, and Beethoven was perhaps not entirely pleased with them. He later wrote to his publisher <a href="/wiki/Breitkopf_%26_H%C3%A4rtel" title="Breitkopf &amp; Härtel">Breitkopf &amp; Härtel</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>You may wish to print another text, as the text like the music was written very quickly ... Still with another set of words I want the word <i>kraft</i> ["strength"] to be kept or one similar to it in its place.</p></blockquote>
<p>As Kalischer et al. observe, the word <i>Kraft</i> "is treated with grand style in the music."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A new German text was written by a German poet and Communist politician <a href="/wiki/Johannes_R._Becher" title="Johannes R. Becher">Johannes R. Becher</a> in 1951, keeping the word <i>Kraft</i> in the same position. Becher's text, inspired by Beethoven's explicit permission to change the lyrics, is an ode to peace that reflects the post-war atmosphere. Becher's lyrics were used in several recordings especially in <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, for example those of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Konwitschny" title="Franz Konwitschny">Franz Konwitschny</a> or <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kegel" title="Herbert Kegel">Herbert Kegel</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Choral_Fantasy_(Beethoven)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1327269900">.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}body.skin-vector-2022 .mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:27em}body.skin-vector-2022 .mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:22.5em}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=upper-alpha]{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=upper-roman]{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-alpha]{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-greek]{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-roman]{list-style-type:lower-roman}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-upper-alpha .references{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-upper-roman .references{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-alpha .references{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-greek .references{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-roman .references{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns" style="column-width: calc( 0.9 * 30em );"><ol class="references">

(1996, 10) identify Kuffner (1780–1846) as "an official at the War Ministry, musically talented poet, author of the text of the Choral Fantasia Op. 80, and of the drama <i>Tarpeja</i>, for which Beethoven supplied incidental music WoO 2, in 1813."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kinderman (1995, 132)</span>

Mähler</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Native name</th><td class="infobox-data"><i>Fantasia</i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Key</th><td href="/wiki/Opus_number" title="Opus number">Opus</a></th><td class="infobox-data">80</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Language</th><td class="infobox-data">German</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Composed</th><td class="infobox-data">1808</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Duration</th><td class="infobox-data">About 20 minutes</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Scoring</th><td class="infobox-data"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1129693374">.mw-parser-output .hlist dl,.mw-parser-output .hlist ol,.mw-parser-output .hlist ul{margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .hlist dd,.mw-parser-output .hlist dt,.mw-parser-output .hlist li{margin:0;display:inline}.mw-parser-output .hlist.inline,.mw-parser-output .hlist.inline dl,.mw-parser-output .hlist ol ol,.mw-parser-output .hlist ol ul,.mw-parser-output .hlist ul dl,.mw-parser-output .hlist ul ol,.mw-parser-output .hlist ul ul{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .hlist .mw-empty-li{display:none}.mw-parser-output .hlist dt::after{content:": "}.mw-parser-output .hlist dd::after,.mw-parser-output .hlist li::after{content:" · ";font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .hlist dt:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist dt li:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist li dd:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist li dt:first-child::before,.mw-parser-output .hlist
